                    }
                }

<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41477"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="The rubber tree genome reveals new insights into rubber production and species adaptation"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Plants 2016 2:6"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2016-05-23"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2016 Macmillan Publishers Limited"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="The Para rubber tree (Hevea brasiliensis) is an economically important tropical tree species that produces natural rubber, an essential industrial raw material. Here we present a high-quality genome assembly of this species (1.37&#8197;Gb, scaffold N50&#8197;=&#8197;1.28 Mb) that covers 93.8% of the genome (1.47&#8197;Gb) and harbours 43,792 predicted protein-coding genes. A striking expansion of the REF/SRPP (rubber elongation factor/small rubber particle protein) gene family and its divergence into several laticifer-specific isoforms seem crucial for rubber biosynthesis. The REF/SRPP family has isoforms with sizes similar to or larger than SRPP1 (204 amino acids) in 17 other plants examined, but no isoforms with similar sizes to REF1 (138 amino acids), the predominant molecular variant. A pivotal point in Hevea evolution was the emergence of REF1, which is located on the surface of large rubber particles that account for 93% of rubber in the latex (despite constituting only 6% of total rubber particles, large and small). The stringent control of ethylene synthesis under active ethylene signalling and response in laticifers resolves a longstanding mystery of ethylene stimulation in rubber production. Our study, which includes the re-sequencing of five other Hevea cultivars and extensive RNA-seq data, provides a valuable resource for functional genomics and tools for breeding elite Hevea cultivars. A high-quality rubber tree genome reveals insights into the evolution of rubber biosynthesis and ethylene stimulation in rubber production. Together with transcriptome data, this study provides valuable data for the research and breeding of rubber trees."/>

/><meta itemprop="address" content="grid.453499.6, 0000 0000 9835 1415, Rubber Research Institute, Chinese Academy of Tropical Agricultural Sciences (CATAS), Danzhou 571737, China" /></span></sup>, </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click"             </div>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>The Para rubber tree (<i>Hevea brasiliensis</i>) is an economically important tropical tree species that produces natural rubber, an essential industrial raw material. Here we present a high-quality genome assembly of this species (1.37 Gb, scaffold N50 = 1.28 Mb) that covers 93.8% of the genome (1.47 Gb) and harbours 43,792 predicted protein-coding genes. A striking expansion of the <i>REF/SRPP</i> (rubber elongation factor/small rubber particle protein) gene family and its divergence into several laticifer-specific isoforms seem crucial for rubber biosynthesis. The REF/SRPP family has isoforms with sizes similar to or larger than SRPP1 (204 amino acids) in 17 other plants examined, but no isoforms with similar sizes to REF1 (138 amino acids), the predominant molecular variant. A pivotal point in <i>Hevea</i> evolution was the emergence of REF1, which is located on the surface of large rubber particles that account for 93% of rubber in the latex (despite constituting only 6% of total rubber particles, large and small). The stringent control of ethylene synthesis under active ethylene signalling and response in laticifers resolves a longstanding mystery of ethylene stimulation in rubber production. Our study, which includes the re-sequencing of five other <i>Hevea</i> cultivars and extensive RNA-seq data, provides a valuable resource for functional genomics and tools for breeding elite <i>Hevea</i> cultivars.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>
                
                    <div class="c-pdf-button__container">
                        
                            
    
    <div class="c-pdf-download u-clear-both">
        <a href="/articles/nplants201673.pdf" class="c-pdf-download__link" data-article-pdf="true" data-readcube-pdf-url="true" data-test="download-pdf" data-draft-ignore="true" data-track="click" data-track-action="download pdf" data-track-label="link" data-track-external download>
            <span>Download PDF</span>
            <svg aria-hidden="true" focusable="false" width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xlink:href="#global-icon-download"/></svg>
        </a>
    </div>
    

                        
                    </div>
                
                
                    <div class="c-article-section__content"><p>The rubber tree (<i>Hevea brasiliensis</i>, hereafter referred to as <i>Hevea</i>) is a member of the spurge family (Euphorbiaceae), along with several other economically important species such as cassava (<i>Manihot esculenta</i>) and the castor oil plant (<i>Ricinus communis</i>). Natural rubber (<i>cis</i>-1, 4-polyisoprene) makes up about one-third of the volume of latex that is essentially cytoplasm of the articulated laticifers in <i>Hevea</i>. The latex is extracted by tapping the bark, a non-destructive method of harvesting that facilitates continual production. As an industrial commodity, natural rubber is an elastomer with physical and chemical properties that cannot be fully matched by synthetic rubber<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="van Beilen, J. B. &amp; Poirier, Y. Establishment of new crops for the production of natural rubber. Trends Biotechnol. 25, 522–529 (2007)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d17e933">1</a></sup>. In contrast to synthetics, the production of natural rubber is sustainable and environment friendly<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Jones, K. Natural rubber as a green commodity—Part II. Rubber Dev. 47, 37–41 (1994)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d17e937">2</a></sup>. The commercial cultivation of <i>Hevea</i>, a native to the Amazon Basin, began in 1896 on a plantation scale in Malaya (now Malaysia) and expanded to other Southeast Asian countries that lead in world natural rubber production today<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Chan, H. in Milestones in Rubber Research (ed. Ong, E. L. ) 2–15 (Malaysian Rubber Board, 2000)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d17e944">3</a></sup>. Decades of selective breeding have resulted in a gradual improvement in rubber productivity, from 650 kg ha<sup>–1</sup> derived from unselected seedlings during the 1920s to 2,500 kg ha<sup>–1</sup> yielded by elite cultivars by the 1990s<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Priyadarshan, P. &amp; Goncalves, P. d.S. Hevea gene pool for breeding. Genet. Resour. Crop Ev. 50, 101–114 (2003)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d17e953">4</a></sup>. Nevertheless, the field production achieved so far is still well below the theoretical yield of 7,000–12,000 kg ha<sup>–1</sup>, as has been suggested for the rubber tree<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Paardekooper, E. in Rubber (eds Webster, C. &amp; Baulkwill, W. ) 349–414 (Longman Scientific and Technical, 1989)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d17e959">5</a></sup>. Meanwhile, conventional rubber breeding has been stagnating in the introduction of high-yield cultivars. The reasons include a narrow genetic basis for exploiting breeding potential and difficulty in introducing wild germplasms because of the genetic burden in removing unfavourable alleles<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Clément-Demange, A., Priyadarshan, P., Hoa, T. T. T. &amp; Venkatachalam, P. Hevea rubber breeding and genetics. Plant Breeding Rev. 29, 177 (2007)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d17e963">6</a></sup>. The incorporation of marker-assisted selection and transgenic techniques offers promise to improve breeding efficiency for latex yield, and sequencing of the <i>Hevea</i> genome would uncover even more avenues leading to this end.</p></div><div class="c-article-section__content"><p>The first draft <i>Hevea</i> genome was released by a Malaysian team<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Rahman, A. Y. et al. Draft genome sequence of the rubber tree Hevea brasiliensis. BMC Genomics 14, 75 (2013)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d17e976">7</a></sup> that was participant to the recent boom in transcriptomic and proteomic studies of the species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Wang, X. et al. Comprehensive proteomics analysis of laticifer latex reveals new insights into ethylene stimulation of natural rubber production. Sci. Rep. 5, 13778 (2015)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d17e980">8</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Chao, J., Chen, Y., Wu, S. &amp; Tian, W. M. Comparative transcriptome analysis of latex from rubber tree clone CATAS8-79 and PR107 reveals new cues for the regulation of latex regeneration and duration of latex flow. BMC Plant Biol. 15, 104 (2015)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d17e983">11</a></sup>. However, its low sequence coverage (<span class="stix">∼</span>13×) and a lack of large insert libraries (such as fosmid- or BAC-based clone libraries) have limited the success of genome assembly (a scaffold N50 size of 2,972 bp), precluding its application for furthering quality research in the field.</p></div><div class="c-article-section__content"><p>Here, we report a high-quality genome assembly of <i>Hevea</i> Reyan7-33-97, an elite cultivar widely planted in China<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Wang, S., Xu, Y., Luo, S. &amp; Gao, X. Adaptability of new desirable rubber clone Reyan7-33-97 in central mountainous areas of Hainan province. Chin. J. Trop. Agr. 29, 1–4 (2009)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d17e993">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Huang, H., Liang, M., Wu, Y., Li, D. &amp; He, J. Selection and breeding of a moderate scale clone SCATC7-33-97. Chin. J. Trop. Crops 15, 1–6 (1994)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d17e996">13</a></sup> based on sequence data from both whole-genome shotgun (WGS) and pooled BAC clones. This assembly contains 7,453 scaffolds (N50 = 1.28 Mb), has a length of 1.37 Gb and covers <span class="stix">∼</span>94% of the predicted genome size (1.46 Gb). Together with analysis of data from re-sequencing five other cultivars and comprehensive transcriptomic surveys, we aim to obtain new insights into the physiology of laticifers and molecular details of rubber biosynthesis, especially in relation to ethylene-stimulated rubber production.</p></div><section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec2">Genome assembly and annotation</h3><p>We assembled the <i>Hevea</i> genome (cultivar Reyan7-33-97) based on 138 Gb (94× genome coverage) processed shotgun sequences (Illumina GA2 and Hiseq 2000; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#MOESM8">Supplementary Table 1</a>). We also generated paired-end reads for five other <i>Hevea</i> cultivars (PR107, Reyan8-79, RRIM600, Wenchang11 and Yunyan77-4) with high-coverage of 38× to 59× (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#MOESM7">Supplementary Fig. 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#MOESM8">Supplementary Table 2</a>). Based on a 17-mer sequence library, the estimated genome size of Reyan7-33-97 is 1.46 Gb, whereas that of the other five cultivars ranges from 1.41 Gb to 1.55 Gb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#MOESM7">Supplementary Fig. 2</a>). To assist with scaffold construction, we generated an additional 55-Gb high-quality mate-pair data (insert sizes from 800 to 10,000 bp; 682× physical coverage; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#MOESM8">Supplementary Table 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#MOESM7">Supplementary Fig. 3</a>), and obtained a preliminary assembly with a scaffold N50 value of 55 kb. We further improved the assembly by adding pooled BAC sequences (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#MOESM7">Supplementary Fig. 4 and Supplementary Note 1</a>) from 47,616 BAC clones (mean insert size = 135 kb) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#MOESM8">Supplementary Table 3</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#MOESM7">Supplementary Fig. 5</a>) that generated pseudo-mate-pair reads of 10 to 200 kb insert lengths to assist scaffolding (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#MOESM7">Supplementary Fig. 6</a>). This yielded a 1.37-Gb genome assembly that covers 93.8% of the estimated genome size, and contains 7,453 scaffolds (N50 = 1.28 Mb) and 84,285 contigs (N50 = 30.6 kb) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#Tab1">Table 1</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Statistics for the <i>Hevea</i> genome and gene annotation.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/nplants201673/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To validate quality of the assembly, we first aligned all <i>Hevea</i> DNA, expressed sequence tag (EST) and protein sequences available in the public domain to show mapping rates of 91.5, 97.9 and 100%, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#MOESM7">Supplementary Figs 7 and 8</a>). Of the 1.11-Gb <i>Hevea</i> genome assembled by the Malaysian group<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Rahman, A. Y. et al. Draft genome sequence of the rubber tree Hevea brasiliensis. BMC Genomics 14, 75 (2013)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d17e1408">7</a></sup>, only 25.2 Mb failed to be aligned to our assembly. The unalignable portions may reflect real sequence differences between cultivars, RRIM600 used by the Malaysian team and Reyan7-33-97 in the present study, as also reflected in the genome size difference (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#MOESM7">Supplementary Fig. 2</a>). Next, we aligned 18 BAC scaffolds with low repetitive sequences to our genome assembly, and yielded alignments from 91.69% to 99.74% (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#MOESM7">Supplementary Fig. 9</a>). Finally, the transcripts we assembled also showed excellent alignment to the genome; of 84,241 transcripts, 98.32% were mapped (transcript coverage &gt; 80% and identity &gt; 99%; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#MOESM8">Supplementary Table 4</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#MOESM7">Supplementary Note 4</a>).</p><p>To assist genome annotation and gene expression analysis, we combined three methods: <i>ab initio</i>, 84,241 unique transcripts (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#MOESM8">Supplementary Table 5</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#MOESM7">Supplementary Fig. 10</a>) and protein homologues (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#MOESM7">Supplementary Fig. 11</a>) to define 43,792 protein-coding genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#Tab1">Table 1</a>), of which 39,919 were found in the NCBI non-redundant protein database (NRPD, Mar. 2015, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#MOESM8">Supplementary Table 6</a>). A gene family survey clustered 16,315 gene families, among which 1,077 are <i>Hevea</i> specific and 10,675 are shared with <i>Manihot esculenta</i>, <i>Ricinus communis</i>, <i>Populus trichocarpa</i> and <i>Linum usitatissimum</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#Fig1">Fig. 1a</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#MOESM7">Supplementary Note 9</a>). In addition, a homologue search for non-coding RNAs predicted 167 ribosomal RNAs, 591 miRNAs, 10 SPPRNAs, 3,445 SnoRNAs, 4 tmRNAs, 697 tRNAs, 219 snRNAs and 217 other types of RNAs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#MOESM8">Supplementary Table 7</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Collinearity and evolution of the Hevea genome."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1: Collinearity and evolution of the <i>Hevea</i> genome.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nplants201673/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnplants.2016.73/MediaObjects/41477_2016_Article_BFnplants201673_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnplants.2016.73/MediaObjects/41477_2016_Article_BFnplants201673_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="611"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p><b>a</b>, A Venn diagram of shared orthologues among five species. Each number represents a gene family number. <b>b</b>, Density distributions of <i>K</i><sub>s</sub> and 4DTv for paralogous genes. The peak values are shown in insets. <i>Hbr</i>, <i>H. brasiliensis</i>; <i>Mes</i>, <i>M. esculenta</i>; <i>Rco</i>, <i>R. communis</i>; <i>Ptr</i>, <i>P. trichocarpa</i>; <i>Lus</i>, <i>L. usitatissimum.</i> <b>c</b>, Collinear region representation of <i>Hbr</i> scaffold0195 to the corresponding <i>Mes</i> and <i>Ptr</i> scaffolds. The outer circle with different colours denotes scaffolds of different species. Each scaffold is scaled, and the scale label is 1 × 10<sup>–6</sup> of actual size. The inner circle shows the genes in the corresponding scaffolds, and genes in positive and negative strands are represented by red and black colours, respectively. The collinear blocks between two species are linked by curved ribbons. <b>d</b>, Phylogenetic tree of 13 species based on orthologues of single-gene families. The number in each node indicates the number of gene families. The number at the root (10,758) represents the number of gene families in the common ancestor. The values above each branch denote gene family gain/loss number at each round of genome duplication after diversifying from the common ancestor. The green numbers below each branch denote speculated divergent time of each node. The clades are marked by four different block colours in the tree: the last one (red) is a monocot species, <i>O. sativa</i>, used as an outgroup; the first three are Rosids clades in dicots, including Fabidae (green), COM (yellow) and Malvidae (brown). Bootstrap values for each node are above 50%.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nplants201673/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Repeat-driven genome expansion</h3><p>We identified 71% of the genome length as repeats (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#Tab1">Table 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#MOESM8">Supplementary Table 8</a>). Among the five species of Malpighiales (<i>Hevea</i>, <i>Manihot, Ricinus</i>, <i>Populus</i> and <i>Linum</i>)<i>, Hevea</i> not only has the largest genome but also the greatest repeat content (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#MOESM8">Supplementary Table 9</a>). Of the repeat sequences, two types of long-terminal repeat retrotransposons (LTR-RTs), <i>Gypsy</i> (691,209 in number) and <i>Copia</i> (114,165), are most abundant, and they total more than half (<span class="stix">∼</span>0.7 Gb; 50.9%) of the genome assembly, substantially higher than that of the other four Malpighiales species. Among the five Malpighiales species, <i>Hevea</i> harbours the largest set (35,079) of LTR-RTs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#MOESM8">Supplementary Table 10</a>), and both <i>Gypsy</i> and <i>Copia</i> show a peak substitution rate around 0.05, suggesting a burst of LTR-RT insertion at that point (of time) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#MOESM7">Supplementary Fig. 12</a>), which occurred five million years (Myr) ago as calculated (see Methods). Similar to <i>Hevea</i>, three other Malpighiales species (<i>Manihot</i>, <i>Ricinus</i> and <i>Populus</i>, with <i>Linum</i> being the exception) have more <i>Gypsy</i> than <i>Copia</i>, and share nearly identical substitution rates for the two elements (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#MOESM7">Supplementary Fig. 12</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Genome duplication, collinearity and phylogeny</h3><p>We explored gene-based collinearity and calculated synonymous substitution rate (<i>K</i><sub>s</sub>) as well as fourfold synonymous third-codon transversion (4DTv) rate among paralogues and orthologues. With the exception of <i>Ricinus</i>, all the other four Malpighiales species display an obvious two-peak pattern for the distributions of Ks/4DTv, with the left sharp peak representing the recent species-specific duplication and the right smooth one representing the eurosid duplication (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#Fig1">Fig. 1b</a>). The most ancient peak, representing γ duplication, is rather obscure and mingled with the right peak. A recent phylogenomic analyses inferred the clades Euphorbiaceae (<i>Hevea</i>, <i>Manihot</i>), Salicaceae (<i>Populus</i>) and Linaceae (<i>Linum</i>) to have emerged 89.9, 58.0 and 39.5 Myr ago, respectively<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Xi, Z. et al. Phylogenomics and a posteriori data partitioning resolve the Cretaceous angiosperm radiation Malpighiales. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 109, 17519–17524 (2012)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d17e1669">14</a></sup>. Hence, <i>Linum</i> is the youngest of the five species, as also reflected by its lower <i>K</i><sub>s</sub> and 4DTv peak values (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#Fig1">Fig. 1b</a>). If the diversification time is assumed to a maximum of 89.9 million years, according to the <i>K</i><sub>s</sub> value of the second peak, the <i>Hevea</i> synonymous substitution constant is estimated as 7.5 × 10<sup>–</sup><sup>9</sup> per site per year. Thus, the recent <i>Hevea</i>-specific duplication might have occurred in <span class="stix">∼</span>15.3 Myr ago, nearly 10 million years earlier than the burst of <i>Hevea</i> LTR insertion. However, we are still not sure whether the two duplication events in <i>Hevea</i> are whole genome duplication because only 30.0% and 5.8% genes are covered by the first and second peaks, respectively. In comparison, the relative rates in <i>Manihot</i>, <i>Populus</i> and <i>Linum</i> are much higher; for example, 92.8% and 56.8% genes are attributable to the two peaks of <i>Populus</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#MOESM8">Supplementary Table 12</a>).</p><p>Plotting collinear regions of <i>Hevea</i> with itself and five other species reveals obvious conserved gene clusters (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#MOESM7">Supplementary Fig. 13</a>). The absence of collinearity with <i>Ricinus</i> is unexpected, which might indicate a lack of species-specific duplication in <i>Ricinus</i> as revealed by its <i>K</i><sub>s</sub>/4DTv distribution pattern (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#Fig1">Fig. 1b</a>). Among <i>Hevea</i>, <i>Manihot</i> and <i>Populus</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#MOESM7">Supplementary Fig. 14</a>), the collinearity size of <i>Hevea</i> is significantly larger than that of <i>Manihot</i> (<i>P</i> = 1.3 × 10<sup>–8</sup>) or <i>Populus</i> (<i>P</i> = 1.79× 10<sup>–7</sup>). The largest collinearity region exists between <i>Hevea</i> scaffold0195 (1,174,012 bp; 108 genes) and <i>Manihot</i> scaffold06656 (752,726 bp; 81 genes) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#Fig1">Fig. 1c</a>), where 71 genes are overall matched. In <i>Populus</i>, four scaffolds are collinear to the <i>Hevea</i> scaffold0195, indicating structural variations between <i>Hevea</i> and <i>Populus</i>.</p><p>To analyse the gene gain–loss event, we used 72 single-copy gene families to construct a maximum likelihood phylogeny between <i>Hevea</i> and 12 other species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#Fig1">Fig. 1d</a>), representing a typical Rosid clade sequenced so far in Eudicots. Taxonomically, <i>Hevea</i>, <i>Manihot</i>, <i>Ricinus</i>, <i>Populus</i> and <i>Linum</i> belong to the Celastrales–Oxalidales–Malpighiales (COM). The phylogenetic placement of the COM clade remains controversial in angiosperms. Here, the COM clade was placed in Malvidae, consistent with a recent study using single- and multicopy nuclear loci<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Sun, M. et al. Deep phylogenetic incongruence in the angiosperm clade Rosidae. Mol. Phylogenet. Evol. 83, 156–166 (2015)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d17e1823">15</a></sup>. Among the 13 species examined, <i>Hevea</i> and rice are representative of the most and fewest gene families, respectively. In most species, the gene family gain is less than gene loss; the only exceptions are in rice and soybean. The divergence time for the Euphorbiaceae family is estimated to be 57.7 Myr ago (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#Fig1">Fig. 1d</a>), more recent than a previously reported dating of 89.9 Myr ago<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Xi, Z. et al. Phylogenomics and a posteriori data partitioning resolve the Cretaceous angiosperm radiation Malpighiales. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 109, 17519–17524 (2012)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d17e1833">14</a></sup>, possibly because of the limited number of Euphorbiaceae species used here.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Genetic diversity among <i>Hevea</i> cultivars</h3><p>The high-quality genome assembly allows us to characterize genetic diversity among the <i>Hevea</i> cultivars Reyan7-33-97 (RY73397), PR107, RRIM600, Wenchang11 (WC11), Reyan8-79 (RY879) and Yunyan77-4 (YY774) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#MOESM8">Supplementary Table 2</a>). Among them, PR107 and RRIM600 are cultivated globally, and the other four are elite cultivars bred and widely planted in China, with PR107 and/or RRIM600 as direct or indirect parents (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#MOESM7">Supplementary Fig. 1</a>).</p><p>The density of single nucleotide polymorphisms (SNPs) contributed by all cultivars averages <span class="stix">∼</span>2 SNPs per kilobase (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#MOESM8">Supplementary Table 12</a>), and 95.1–95.6% SNPs occur in non-coding regions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#MOESM8">Supplementary Table 13</a>). YY774 has the most variety-specific SNPs (442,278), whereas WC11 has the least (132,006) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#Fig2">Fig. 2a</a>). Phylogeny based on SNPs shows the genetic relationship among the cultivars (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#Fig2">Fig. 2b</a>) is consistent with their breeding history (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#MOESM7">Supplementary Fig. 1</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Genetic diversity among six Hevea cultivars."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2: Genetic diversity among six <i>Hevea</i> cultivars.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nplants201673/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnplants.2016.73/MediaObjects/41477_2016_Article_BFnplants201673_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnplants.2016.73/MediaObjects/41477_2016_Article_BFnplants201673_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="772"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a</b>, SNP number distribution in six cultivars. <b>b</b>, Phylogenetic analysis of cultivars based on SNPs and frequency distribution. Using homology SNP sites of the cultivars, the tree is constructed by MEGA5.1 with the Neighbour-Joining model and the bootstrap test (1,000 replicates). The series of plots on the right show SNP density (SNP per kb) and frequencies. SNP frequency is calculated based on a 50-kb sliding window in 1-kb steps. <b>c</b>, Gene Ontology (GO) enrichment in biological process for genes located in core SNP desert using GOEAST. Note that two biological processes, defence response (DR) and respiratory electron transport chain (RETC), are most enriched. LBP, leucine biosynthetic process; ASCPT, ATP synthesis coupled proton transport; CMO, cellulose microfibril organization; CG, cell growth.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nplants201673/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Low genetic diversity regions or ‘SNP deserts’ often indicate selective sweeps<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Kaplan, N. L., Hudson, R. &amp; Langley, C. The “hitchhiking effect” revisited. Genetics 123, 887–899 (1989)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d17e1912">16</a></sup> and relate to domestication in many major crop species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Wang, L. et al. SNP deserts of Asian cultivated rice: genomic regions under domestication. J. Evol. Biol. 22, 751–761 (2009)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d17e1916">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Tenaillon, M. I., U'Ren, J., Tenaillon, O. &amp; Gaut, B. S. Selection versus demography: a multilocus investigation of the domestication process in maize. Mol. Biol. Evol. 21, 1214–1225 (2004)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d17e1919">18</a></sup>. All re-sequenced <i>Hevea</i> cultivars exhibit a unimodal pattern in the distribution of SNPs with a single low-SNP peak at &lt;1 SNP per kilobase (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#Fig2">Fig. 2b</a>), contrasting with bimodal curves observed in rice and date palm, where the low-SNP peak reflects trait selection in cultivation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Wang, L. et al. SNP deserts of Asian cultivated rice: genomic regions under domestication. J. Evol. Biol. 22, 751–761 (2009)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d17e1929">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Al-Mssallem, I. S. et al. Genome sequence of the date palm Phoenix dactylifera L. Nature Commun. 4, 2274 (2013)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d17e1932">19</a></sup>. The SNP deserts account for 42% of the <i>Hevea</i> genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#MOESM8">Supplementary Table 14</a>), a value significantly higher than that reported in rice (8%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Wang, L. et al. SNP deserts of Asian cultivated rice: genomic regions under domestication. J. Evol. Biol. 22, 751–761 (2009)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d17e1943">17</a></sup> or date palm (18%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Al-Mssallem, I. S. et al. Genome sequence of the date palm Phoenix dactylifera L. Nature Commun. 4, 2274 (2013)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d17e1947">19</a></sup>. Compared with the genome average, the SNP desert has a higher <i>K</i><sub>a</sub>/<i>K</i><sub>s</sub> (nonsynonymous/synonymous substitution) ratio (1.72 versus 1.27) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#MOESM8">Supplementary Table 14</a>). The low-SNP peak in various cultivars might be attributed to natural selection; artificial selection remains a possibility, although to a lesser extent due to a short domestication history of <i>Hevea</i> (since the late nineteenth century).</p><p>About half of SNP deserts (13,127 blocks, 261,150 kb) are conserved in all six cultivars (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#MOESM8">Supplementary Table 15</a>), defined as shared or core SNP deserts representing most sequence signatures left mainly by natural selection after <i>Hevea</i> speciation. There are 3,820 genes (8.7% of the total genes) located in the core SNP desert (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#MOESM8">Supplementary Table 16</a>) that are most enriched in respiratory electron transport chain and defence response functions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#Fig2">Fig. 2c</a>). Of particular interest is the fact that the single most highly expressed gene in latex—by a wide margin—<i>REF1</i> resides in the core SNP desert (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#MOESM8">Supplementary Tables 16 and 20</a>). In this regard, the sister gene to <i>REF1</i>, <i>SRPP1</i>, is noteworthy by its absence in the SNP desert. Meanwhile, both genes showed the least sequence diversity among the six <i>Hevea</i> cultivars examined, suggesting the importance of their conservation in this species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#MOESM8">Supplementary Table 17</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Rubber biosynthesis and expansion of the REF/SRPP family</h3><p>In the laticifer network of <i>Hevea</i>, natural rubber is synthesized by a sequential condensation of isopentenyl diphosphates (IPPs) in <i>cis</i>-configuration to the priming allylic molecules (initiators)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="d'Auzac, J. et al. The regulation of cis-polyisoprene production (natural rubber) from Hevea brasiliensis. Recent Res. Dev. Plant Physiol. 1, 273–332 (1997)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d17e2015">20</a></sup>. By convention, genes involved in the synthesis of IPP to the final rubber polymer are termed as rubber biosynthesis genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Chow, K. S. et al. Insights into rubber biosynthesis from transcriptome analysis of Hevea brasiliensis latex. J. Exp. Bot. 58, 2429–2440 (2007)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d17e2019">21</a></sup>. From our genome assembly, 84 rubber biosynthesis genes from 20 gene families were identified (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#MOESM8">Supplementary Table 18</a>), including 18 in the cytosolic mevalonate (MVA) pathway and 22 in the plastidic 2-C-methyl-D-erythritol-4-phosphate (MEP) pathway for IPP synthesis, 15 for initiator synthesis in the cytosol and 39 for ‘rubber elongation’ on rubber particles (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#Fig3">Fig. 3a</a>). Of the 24 MEP genes, only two <i>DXS</i> (1-deoxy-D-xylulose 5-phosphate synthase) genes (<i>DXS7</i> and <i>DXS10</i>) show substantial and preferential expression in latex (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#Fig3">Fig. 3a</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#MOESM8">Supplementary Table 18</a>). In contrast, at least one gene for each MVA pathway enzyme shows latex-biased abundant expression, supporting the proposition that it is the MVA rather than the MEP pathway that is involved in rubber biosynthesis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Sando, T. et al. Cloning and characterization of the 2-C-methyl-D-erythritol 4-phosphate (MEP) pathway genes of a natural-rubber producing plant, Hevea brasiliensis. Biosci. Biotech. Bioch. 72, 2903–2917 (2008)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d17e2046">22</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Sando, T. et al. Cloning and characterization of mevalonate pathway genes in a natural rubber producing plant, Hevea brasiliensis. Biosci. Biotech. Bioch. 72, 2049–2060 (2008)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d17e2049">23</a></sup>. Three gene families, <i>REF/SRPP</i>, <i>CPT</i> (<i>cis</i>-prenyltransferase) and <i>DXS</i>, have more than ten genes. Recently, a homologue of the human Nogo-B receptor was demonstrated to be essential for rubber biosynthesis in dandelion<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Epping, J. et al. A rubber transferase activator is necessary for natural rubber biosynthesis in dandelion. Nature Plants 1,15048 (2015)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d17e2065">24</a></sup>. However, blast searching yielded no significant hits in our genome assembly or the public <i>Hevea</i> sequences, indicating a discrepancy in the mechanisms of rubber biosynthesis between these two species.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Rubber biosynthesis and expansion of the REF/SRPP gene family in Hevea."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3: Rubber biosynthesis and expansion of the <i>REF</i>/<i>SRPP</i> gene family in <i>Hevea</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nplants201673/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnplants.2016.73/MediaObjects/41477_2016_Article_BFnplants201673_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnplants.2016.73/MediaObjects/41477_2016_Article_BFnplants201673_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="1382"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p><b>a</b>, The rubber biosynthesis pathway and expression profiles (reads per kilobase per million reads mapped; RPKM) of the genes involved in rubber biosynthesis. Lx, latex; Bk, bark; Lf, leaf; Rt, root; FF, female flower; MF, male flower. <b>b</b>, Phylogeny of the <i>REF/SRPP</i> gene family. Full-length coding sequences of the 18 <i>REF</i>/<i>SRPPs</i> are aligned with MAFFT v7.205. The tree is constructed by using MEGA5.1, with the Neighbour-Joining model and bootstrap test with 1,000 replicates. <b>c</b>, Genomic location of the <i>Hevea</i> <i>REF</i>/<i>SRPP</i> genes. Scaffolds are represented as solid bars with their names on the left and length scale on the bottom. Note that most of the <i>REF/SRPP</i> genes, including the four laticifer-specific ones (<i>REF1</i>, <i>SRPP1</i>, <i>REF3</i> and <i>REF7</i>), are located in a single scaffold (Scaffold1222).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nplants201673/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The 18-member REF/SRPP family of <i>Hevea</i> is the largest compared with the other 17 sequenced plants examined this study (10 at most) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#MOESM8">Supplementary Table 19</a>), indicating an obvious expansion of this family in <i>Hevea</i>. The gene number of the REF/SRPP family does not seem to correlate with either genome size or polyploidy of a given species. For example, only four <i>REF/SRPP</i> genes are identified in the hexaploid <i>Triticum aestivum</i> genome (17 Gb) but six in the small diploid genome of <i>Populus trichocarpa</i> (480 Mb). When considering other rubber-producing plants, eight REF/SRPP genes are identified in <i>Lactuca sativa</i> and six in limited EST/mRNA sequences of <i>Parthenium argentatum</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#MOESM8">Supplementary Table 19</a>). These results suggest a possible link between expansion of the REF/SRPP family and the ability to produce rubber.</p><p>A close involvement of REF and SRPP in rubber synthesis has been proposed based on <i>in vitro</i> rubber biosynthesis assays<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Oh, S. K. et al. Isolation, characterization, and functional analysis of a novel cDNA clone encoding a small rubber particle protein from Hevea brasiliensis. J. Biol. Chem. 274, 17132–17138 (1999)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d17e2183">25</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Dennis, M. S. &amp; Light, D. R. Rubber elongation factor from Hevea brasiliensis. Identification, characterization, and role in rubber biosynthesis. J. Biol. Chem. 264, 18608–18617 (1989)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d17e2186">26</a></sup>, and a positive correlation of <i>REF</i> expression with rubber yield<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Priya, P., Venkatachalam, P. &amp; Thulaseedharan, A. Differential expression pattern of rubber elongation factor (REF) mRNA transcripts from high and low yielding clones of rubber tree (Hevea brasiliensis Muell. Arg.). Plant Cell Rep. 26, 1833–1838 (2007)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d17e2193">27</a></sup>. Recent transgenic studies in <i>Taraxacum brevicorniculatum</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Hillebrand, A. et al. Down-regulation of small rubber particle protein expression affects integrity of rubber particles and rubber content in Taraxacum brevicorniculatum. PloS ONE 7, e41874 (2012)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d17e2199">28</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Laibach, N., Hillebrand, A., Twyman, R. M., Prufer, D. &amp; Schulze Gronover, C. Identification of a Taraxacum brevicorniculatum rubber elongation factor protein that is localized on rubber particles and promotes rubber biosynthesis. Plant J. 82, 609–620 (2015)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d17e2202">29</a></sup> also reveal their essential role in rubber biosynthesis. REF and SRPP share a conserved REF motif that is also retained in several stress-related or lipid droplet-associated proteins in plant cells<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Oh, S. K. et al. Isolation, characterization, and functional analysis of a novel cDNA clone encoding a small rubber particle protein from Hevea brasiliensis. J. Biol. Chem. 274, 17132–17138 (1999)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d17e2207">25</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Berthelot, K. et al. Rubber elongation factor (REF), a major allergen component in Hevea brasiliensis latex has amyloid properties. PloS ONE 7, e48065 (2012)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d17e2210">30</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Gidda, S. K. et al. Lipid droplet-associated proteins (LDAPs) are involved in the compartmentalization of lipophilic compounds in plant cells. Plant Signal. Behav. 8, e27141 (2013)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d17e2213">31</a></sup>. Based on the presence of a carboxy (C) terminus found in SRPP and its absence in REF<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Oh, S. K. et al. Isolation, characterization, and functional analysis of a novel cDNA clone encoding a small rubber particle protein from Hevea brasiliensis. J. Biol. Chem. 274, 17132–17138 (1999)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d17e2217">25</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Berthelot, K. et al. Rubber elongation factor (REF), a major allergen component in Hevea brasiliensis latex has amyloid properties. PloS ONE 7, e48065 (2012)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d17e2220">30</a></sup>, and the similarities among protein sequences, the 18 <i>Hevea</i> REF/SRPP genes are named <i>SRPP1</i> to <i>10</i> and <i>REF1</i> to <i>8</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#MOESM7">Supplementary Fig. 15</a>). Of these, REF1 (138 aa) and SRPP1 (204 aa) are the two most abundant isoforms (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#MOESM8">Supplementary Table 18</a>), and correspond, respectively, to the well characterized REF and SRPP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Oh, S. K. et al. Isolation, characterization, and functional analysis of a novel cDNA clone encoding a small rubber particle protein from Hevea brasiliensis. J. Biol. Chem. 274, 17132–17138 (1999)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d17e2246">25</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Dennis, M. S. &amp; Light, D. R. Rubber elongation factor from Hevea brasiliensis. Identification, characterization, and role in rubber biosynthesis. J. Biol. Chem. 264, 18608–18617 (1989)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d17e2249">26</a></sup>. In the other 17 plants examined, the REF/SRPP family has isoforms with sizes similar to or larger than SRPP1, but no isoforms with similar size to REF1. The uniqueness of REF1 is highlighted by an alignment of REF/SRPP proteins from different plants (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#MOESM7">Supplementary Fig. 16</a>).</p><p>The 18 REF/SRPP genes exhibit distinct expression patterns in seven <i>Hevea</i> tissues (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#Fig3">Fig. 3a</a>). Four isoforms, <i>REF1</i>, <i>SRPP1</i>, <i>REF3</i> and <i>REF7,</i> have striking RPKM values of &gt;7,000 in latex, this being especially true of <i>REF1</i> (38,999). In total they account for 96.8% of expression of the REF/SRPP gene family in latex (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#MOESM8">Supplementary Table 18</a>). In addition, their expression in latex is over tenfold higher than in any other tissue. Considering the fact that all other tissues also contain laticifers and small amounts of latex, we think that the expression of these genes in the tissues may have arisen from latex itself, and <i>REF1</i>, <i>SRPP1</i>, <i>REF3</i> and <i>REF7</i> are thus essentially laticifer-specific genes. When compared with all the genes expressed in latex, these four <i>REF/SRPP</i> genes rank among the top, with <i>REF1</i> ranked first, <i>SRPP1</i> sixth, <i>REF3</i> ninth and <i>REF7</i> the twelfth (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#MOESM8">Supplementary Table 20</a>).</p><p>Phylogenetically, the <i>Hevea REF/SRPP</i> genes are classified into two major groups, with 14 in group I and four in group II (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#Fig3">Fig. 3b</a>). Of the group II genes, three (<i>SRPP4</i>, <i>6</i> and <i>10</i>) are not expressed or expressed at very low levels in latex, and the remaining <i>SRPP7</i> shows somewhat constitutive low expression across different tissues (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#Fig3">Fig. 3a</a>), therefore precluding the active participation of group II genes in rubber biosynthesis. group I genes are further divided into two distinct clades, 13 in clade 1 and only <i>SRPP2</i> in clade 2. <i>SRPP2</i> exhibited moderate expression across diverse tissues, excluding its special function in laticifers. Interestingly, all latex-specific isoforms (<i>REF1</i>, <i>SRPP1</i>, <i>REF3</i> and <i>REF7</i>) are clustered in clade 1. When the REF/SRPPs from <i>Hevea</i> and 11 other plants were investigated for their phylogenetic relationship, all the 13 clade 1 isoforms were clustered into an independent clade whereas the remainder were scattered together with the homologues from other plants into different clades (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#MOESM7">Supplementary Fig. 17</a>). These results suggest that the clade 1 <i>REF/SRPP</i> genes might have evolved independently towards functioning in rubber biosynthesis. The <i>Hevea</i> REF/SRPP family was further surveyed for their genomic location. Of particular note, 12 of the 13 clade 1 REF/SRPPs including the four laticifer-specific genes, are located in a single 205-kb Scaffold1222 whereas the remaining six are scattered into six different scaffolds (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#Fig3">Fig. 3c</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#MOESM7">Supplementary Fig. 18</a>). <i>SRPP1</i> and the parallel precursor for <i>REF1</i> appear to have evolved from the same ancestral <i>SRPP</i> gene, but the emergence of <i>REF1</i> was a more recent event (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#Fig3">Fig. 3b</a>, upper part of clade 1).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Active ethylene signalling and response in laticifers</h3><p>Since latex production is greatly increased by the ethylene-releasing agent ethephon, we identified 509 differentially expressed genes (DEGs) in latex that respond to ethylene treatment (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#Fig4">Fig. 4a</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#MOESM8">Supplementary Table 21</a>). About one-quarter of the 342 annotated DEGs (&gt;2-fold expressional change) are transcription factors (53) and protein kinases (31) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#MOESM8">Supplementary Tables 22 and 23</a>). Both gene groups are essential in ethylene signal transduction and response in higher plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Yang, C., Lu, X., Ma, B., Chen, S. Y. &amp; Zhang, J. S. Ethylene signaling in rice and Arabidopsis: conserved and diverged aspects. Mol. Plant 8, 495–505 (2015)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d17e2413">32</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Klee, H. J. Ethylene signal transduction. Moving beyond Arabidopsis. Plant Physiol. 135, 660–667 (2004)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d17e2416">33</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Ethylene stimulation of rubber production in Hevea."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 4: Ethylene stimulation of rubber production in <i>Hevea</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nplants201673/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnplants.2016.73/MediaObjects/41477_2016_Article_BFnplants201673_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnplants.2016.73/MediaObjects/41477_2016_Article_BFnplants201673_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="1355"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Expression dynamics of differentially expressed genes (DEGs) within 24 h of ethylene treatment. The expression levels (RPKM) of the 509 DEGs are normalized based on the <i>Z</i>-score method (scale bar). The DEGs are divided into six clades; two (clades I and II) of them are downregulated genes whereas the remainder (clades III to VI) are upregulated. <b>b</b>, A model summarizing the mechanisms of ethylene stimulation of rubber production. The red and green text used for genes and enzymes denotes that their expression/activity is activated and inhibited by ethylene, respectively. The red background of molecules or events indicates that their quantity or strength is boosted by ethylene. Suc, sucrose; Met, methionine; ROS, reactive oxygen species; NIN, neutral invertase; GS, glutamine synthetase.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nplants201673/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To understand ethylene-dependent mechanisms, we first identified the genes responsible for ethylene biosynthesis in <i>Hevea</i> and examined their expression patterns. The three enzymes that act sequentially to synthesize ethylene from methionine, that is <i>S</i>-adenosyl-<span class="u-small-caps">l-</span>methionine synthase (SAMS), 1-aminocyclopropane-1-carboxylic acid (ACC) synthase (ACS) and ACC oxidase (ACO)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Yang, S. F. &amp; Hoffman, N. E. Ethylene biosynthesis and its regulation in higher plants. Ann. Rev. Plant Physiol. 35, 155–189 (1984)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d17e2464">34</a></sup> have 8, 14 and 16 genes respectively in <i>Hevea</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#MOESM8">Supplementary Table 24</a>). However, the expression of these gene families, especially that of <i>SAMS</i> (&gt;fivefold) and <i>ACO</i> (&gt;10-fold) is much lower in latex than other tissues. Such low expression or a lack of expression has been noticed recently for nine <i>ACO</i> genes in <i>Hevea</i> latex<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Zhu, J. H., Xu, J., Chang, W. J. &amp; Zhang, Z. L. Isolation and molecular characterization of 1-aminocyclopropane-1-carboxylic acid synthase genes in Hevea brasiliensis. Int. J. Mol. Sci. 16, 4136–4149 (2015)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d17e2487">35</a></sup>. These results, together with the low oxygen condition in latex<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Tupy, J. in Physiology of Rubber Tree Latex (eds d'Auzac, J., Jacob, J-L &amp; Chrestin, H. ) 179–218 (CRC, 1989)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d17e2492">36</a></sup> and the requirement of oxygen by ACO to produce ethylene<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Yang, S. F. &amp; Hoffman, N. E. Ethylene biosynthesis and its regulation in higher plants. Ann. Rev. Plant Physiol. 35, 155–189 (1984)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d17e2496">34</a></sup>, point out a weak ethylene-synthesizing ability in laticifers. In addition, ethylene treatment had little effect on the expression of ACS, the rate-limiting enzyme of ethylene biosynthesis in latex (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#MOESM8">Supplementary Table 24</a>). The primary ethylene signalling components, namely <i>ETR</i>, <i>CTR1</i>, <i>EIN2</i> and <i>EIN3/EIL1</i>, were investigated for their expression in latex in response to ethylene treatment (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#MOESM8">Supplementary Table 25</a>). Four out of the eight receptor genes (<i>ETR</i>) showed enhanced expression after ethylene treatment, indicating an ethylene response triggered in laticifers since increased expression has been observed for one or more <i>ETRs</i> in every known ethylene response<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Klee, H. J. Ethylene signal transduction. Moving beyond Arabidopsis. Plant Physiol. 135, 660–667 (2004)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d17e2525">33</a></sup>. Of particular interest, expressions of two <i>EIN2s</i> and one <i>EIL1</i> were apparently boosted in latex after ethylene treatment. As the central transducer and master transcription factors in ethylene signalling, <i>EIN2</i> and <i>EIN3/EIL1</i> genes are mainly controlled by ethylene at a post-translational level<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Yang, C., Lu, X., Ma, B., Chen, S. Y. &amp; Zhang, J. S. Ethylene signaling in rice and Arabidopsis: conserved and diverged aspects. Mol. Plant 8, 495–505 (2015)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d17e2542">32</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Klee, H. J. Ethylene signal transduction. Moving beyond Arabidopsis. Plant Physiol. 135, 660–667 (2004)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d17e2545">33</a></sup>. Hitherto, none of these genes identified in other plants has been found to be transcriptionally regulated by ethylene<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Yang, C., Lu, X., Ma, B., Chen, S. Y. &amp; Zhang, J. S. Ethylene signaling in rice and Arabidopsis: conserved and diverged aspects. Mol. Plant 8, 495–505 (2015)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d17e2549">32</a></sup>. The transcriptional enhancement of <i>EIN2</i> and <i>EIL1</i> in latex suggests an active ethylene signalling in laticifers.</p><p>Downstream of the primary ethylene signalling pathway, ethylene-responsive element binding factors (ERFs) are implicated. A total of 225 <i>Hevea</i> AP2/ERF members, including 181 ERFs, 35 AP2s, 7 RAVs and 2 Soloists (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#MOESM8">Supplementary Table 26</a>) were identified, representing the largest assemblage in this grouping as compared to other plants (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#MOESM8">Supplementary Table 27</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Piyatrakul, P. et al. Sequence and expression analyses of ethylene response factors highly expressed in latex cells from Hevea brasiliensis. PloS ONE 9, e99367 (2014)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d17e2572">37</a></sup>. Nearly one-tenth of the AP2/ERF members showed a more than twofold change in expression after ethylene treatment; 10 of them were upregulated and 11 downregulated (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#MOESM8">Supplementary Table 28</a>). Interestingly, of the seven downregulated <i>ERF</i> genes, four belong to repressor subgroups (ERF-IIa and ERF-VIIIa) of ethylene response<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Nakano, T., Suzuki, K., Fujimura, T. &amp; Shinshi, H. Genome-wide analysis of the ERF gene family in Arabidopsis and rice. Plant Physiol. 140, 411–432 (2006)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d17e2583">38</a></sup>. The depression of their expression is expected to be beneficial for activating downstream ethylene response.</p><p>Ethylene treatment triggers a number of stimulated downstream biochemical cascades in relation to latex production: sugar loading and its catabolism<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Liu, J. P., Xia, Z. Q., Tian, X. Y. &amp; Li, Y. J. Transcriptome sequencing and analysis of rubber tree (Hevea brasiliensis Muell.) to discover putative genes associated with tapping panel dryness (TPD). BMC Genomics 16, 398 (2015)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d17e2590">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Tupy, J. in Physiology of Rubber Tree Latex (eds d'Auzac, J., Jacob, J-L &amp; Chrestin, H. ) 179–218 (CRC, 1989)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d17e2593">36</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Tang, C. et al. The sucrose transporter HbSUT3 plays an active role in sucrose loading to laticifer and rubber productivity in exploited trees of Hevea brasiliensis (para rubber tree). Plant Cell Environ. 33, 1708–1720 (2010)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d17e2596">39</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Dusotoit-Coucaud, A. et al. Ethylene stimulation of latex yield depends on the expression of a sucrose transporter (HbSUT1B) in rubber tree (Hevea brasiliensis). Tree Physiol. 30, 1586–1598 (2010)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d17e2599">40</a></sup>, water uptake<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Tungngoen, K. et al. Involvement of HbPIP2;1 and HbTIP1;1 aquaporins in ethylene stimulation of latex yield through regulation of water exchanges between inner liber and latex cells in Hevea brasiliensis. Plant Physiol. 151, 843–856 (2009)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d17e2603">41</a></sup>, energy availability<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Amalou, Z., Bangratz, J. &amp; Chrestin, H. Ethrel (ethylene releaser)-induced increases in the adenylate pool and transtonoplast delta pH within Hevea latex cells. Plant Physiol. 98, 1270–1276 (1992)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d17e2607">42</a></sup>, cytosolic alkalinization<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Amalou, Z., Bangratz, J. &amp; Chrestin, H. Ethrel (ethylene releaser)-induced increases in the adenylate pool and transtonoplast delta pH within Hevea latex cells. Plant Physiol. 98, 1270–1276 (1992)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d17e2611">42</a></sup>, nitrogen assimilation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Pujade-Renaud, V. et al. Ethylene-Induced increase in glutamine synthetase activity and mRNA levels in Hevea brasiliensis latex cells. Plant Physiol. 105, 127–132 (1994)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d17e2615">43</a></sup> and defence responses<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Putranto, R. A. et al. Ethylene response factors are controlled by multiple harvesting stresses in Hevea brasiliensis. PloS ONE 10, e0123618 (2015)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d17e2620">44</a></sup>. Many genes implicated in these events were among the DEGs identified in this study (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#MOESM8">Supplementary Tables 22 and 23</a>), but not the rubber biosynthesis genes, which is consistent with the previous proposition that ethylene has little direct effect on genes in rubber biosynthesis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Zhu, J. &amp; Zhang, Z. Ethylene stimulation of latex production in Hevea brasiliensis. Plant Signal. Behav. 4, 1072–1074 (2009)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d17e2627">45</a></sup>. The defence response genes involved in producing and scavenging reactive oxygen species (ROS) were further analysed for their importance in the functioning of laticifers<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="d'Auzac, J. et al. The regulation of cis-polyisoprene production (natural rubber) from Hevea brasiliensis. Recent Res. Dev. Plant Physiol. 1, 273–332 (1997)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d17e2631">20</a></sup>. Three DEGs in ROS production were identified, among which an <span class="u-small-caps">l</span>-ascorbate oxidase (scaffold2147_8831) and an NADPH oxidase (scaffold0143_441741) appeared upregulated, whereas a lipoxygenase (scaffold4250_5149) was downregulated (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#MOESM8">Supplementary Tables 22 and 23</a>). Of the six ROS-scavenging DEGs, five that include two thioredoxins (scaffolds0444_166127 and 0520_1896151), a glutaredoxin (scaffold4986_4172), a caleosin-related peroxygenase (scaffold1473_35058) and polyamine oxidase (scaffold0036_2884533) were upregulated, and one, a thioredoxin (scaffold0794_475278), was downregulated. Both the number of DEGs involved in ROS scavenging and their expression levels after ethylene treatment were much higher than those involved in ROS production.
