We illustrate in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#Fig4">Fig. 4b</a> the mechanisms underlying ethylene stimulation of latex production in <i>Hevea</i>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec8" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec8-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec8">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec8-content"><p>First, an effort to improve genome assembly is especially meaningful in plant genomes with high repeat content<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Feuillet, C., Leach, J. E., Rogers, J., Schnable, P. S. &amp; Eversole, K. Crop genome sequencing: lessons and rationales. Trends Plant Sci. 16, 77–88 (2011)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d17e2657">46</a></sup>. The <i>Hevea</i> genome contains 71% repetitive content and our BAC-pool sequencing allowed an over 20-fold increase in scaffold N50 length, which can be exploited in sequencing other repeat-rich large plant genomes. Second, expansion and divergence of the REF/SRPP family and its correlation with rubber biosynthesis leads to new insights into the physiology of rubber-producing laticifers. Third, an in-depth RNA-seq analysis assisted by the high-quality genome assembly has allowed us to gain new understanding of the mechanisms underlying ethylene stimulation of rubber production.</p><p>What sets the rubber tree apart from the numerous other rubber-bearing plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Polhamus, L. G. Rubber. (Leonard Hill, London, 1962)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d17e2667">47</a></sup> is its ability to produce prodigious amounts of rubber. In this regard, the <i>Hevea</i> genome provides a good resource to understand how the tree has managed to accomplish this uncommon feat. An appraisal of data from the genome points to the <i>REF/SRPP</i> gene family, which encompasses the most highly expressed genes in the latex. The divergence of this gene family into several laticifer-specific abundant isoforms suggests extraordinary selection pressure in play. Rubber contained in latex provides <i>Hevea</i> with a protective function against boring pests (such as beetles) with its coagulating ability to entrap them in the exuding latex which then self-seals the wound<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Sharples, A. The laticiferous system of Hevea brasiliensis and its protective function. Ann. Bot. 32, 247–251 (1918)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d17e2680">48</a></sup>. From the viewpoint of evolutionary advantage to the species, this is the clear benefit that the rubber tree receives. From the anthropological perspective, this adaptation has fortuitous consequences in that it provides mankind with the building blocks of a global industry.</p><p>A search for <i>REF</i> and <i>SRPP</i> in the <i>Hevea</i> SNP desert is instructive. <i>REF</i> heads the list in gene expression in the SNP desert, implying that it is an active gene that has descended from a recent, single selection of an ancestral sequence. On the other hand, the significance of <i>SRPP</i> in relation to the SNP desert lies in its absence. The phylogenetic dendrogram in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#Fig3">Fig. 3b</a> offers an explanation. An <i>SRPP</i> isoform is an ancestral protein in the <i>REF/SRPP</i> gene family. A mutation event occurred relatively recently whereby <i>SRPP</i> was truncated to give rise to <i>REF</i>. Deletion of the <i>SRPP</i> C terminus that resulted in <i>REF</i> isoforms appears to have occurred more than once in the rubber tree's evolutionary history, but the isoforms that prevail in modern <i>Hevea</i> are <i>REF1</i> and <i>SRPP1.</i> The late appearance of <i>REF</i> also explains why, among the other 17 plants examined, the <i>REF/SRPP</i> family has isoform sizes similar to or larger than <i>SRPP1</i>, but none with similar size to <i>REF1</i>. The breakout event that produced <i>REF</i> is important to the modern <i>Hevea</i>'s capacity for high rubber production. <i>Hevea</i> is unique in its high rubber production arguably because <i>REF</i> is unique to <i>Hevea.</i></p><p>There are two classes of rubber particles in <i>Hevea</i> latex: the large particles (LRPs) with REF located on their surfaces and the small particles (SRPs) that are coated with SRPP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Oh, S. K. et al. Isolation, characterization, and functional analysis of a novel cDNA clone encoding a small rubber particle protein from Hevea brasiliensis. J. Biol. Chem. 274, 17132–17138 (1999)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d17e2768">25</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Berthelot, K. et al. Rubber particle proteins, HbREF and HbSRPP, show different interactions with model membranes. BBA-Biomembranes 1838, 287–299 (2014)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d17e2771">49</a></sup>. SRPs are far superior in number, accounting for 94% of all rubber particles in the latex, whereas LRPs constitute only the remaining 6% of the rubber particles. However, it is precisely this 6% of rubber particles by number that makes up 93% of the rubber by volume in the latex<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Yeang, H., Yip, E. &amp; Hamzah, S. Characterisation of Zone 1 and Zone 2 rubber particles in Hevea brasiliensis latex. J. Nat. Rubb. Res. 10, 108–123 (1995)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d17e2775">50</a></sup>. <i>REF1</i> might contribute to rubber biosynthesis by facilitating the biogenesis of LRPs or the ‘growing’ of SRPs to LRPs. Two pieces of evidence support this presumption: (1) the amount of REF1 protein in the whole latex has been found to be proportional to the rubber content<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Dennis, M. S. &amp; Light, D. R. Rubber elongation factor from Hevea brasiliensis. Identification, characterization, and role in rubber biosynthesis. J. Biol. Chem. 264, 18608–18617 (1989)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d17e2782">26</a></sup>; (2) <i>REF1</i> gene expression was reported to correlate with yield levels of <i>Hevea</i> cultivars<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Priya, P., Venkatachalam, P. &amp; Thulaseedharan, A. Differential expression pattern of rubber elongation factor (REF) mRNA transcripts from high and low yielding clones of rubber tree (Hevea brasiliensis Muell. Arg.). Plant Cell Rep. 26, 1833–1838 (2007)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d17e2793">27</a></sup>. Taking a hypothetical stance, if REF had not evolved to facilitate the formation of LRPs, the typical tapped latex that has a dry rubber content (drc) of 33% would have less than 3% drc for the same number of SRPs per unit volume. In other words, the entire natural rubber industry is essentially founded on a very small proportion of rubber particles that are LRPs.</p><p>In <i>Hevea</i> planting, the ethylene generating compound, ethephon, is commonly applied to the bark of <i>Hevea</i> to lengthen the flow duration and to aid latex regeneration after tapping. Although bearing a weak capability for ethylene synthesis that is little affected by ethylene treatment too, the laticifers reveal an active ethylene signalling capability as evidenced by transcriptional accentuation of the ethylene signal receptor and transduction genes on ethylene stimulation. The low ethylene-synthesizing ability in laticifers, but an active ethylene signalling mechanism that is responsive to exogenous ethylene could shed new light on how ethylene triggers a significant increase in latex flow and rubber production.</p><p>The data from this study, together with other public resources, pave a way for whole genome association studies, germplasm improvement and genetic modification of <i>Hevea</i> to meet increasing global demand for natural rubber.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec9" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec9-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec9">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec9-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Plant materials</h3><p>Genomic DNA was prepared from young leaves of six <i>Hevea</i> cultivars (Reyan7-33-97, PR107, RRIM600, Reyan8-79, Wenchang11 and Yunyan77-4) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#MOESM7">Supplementary Fig. 1</a>) using the CTAB method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Allen, G. C., Flores-Vergara, M. A., Krasynanski, S., Kumar, S. &amp; Thompson, W. F. A modified protocol for rapid DNA isolation from plant tissues using cetyltrimethylammonium bromide. Nature Protoc. 1, 2320–2325 (2006)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d17e2832">51</a></sup>. RNA samples were prepared from mature leaf, male flower, female flower, mature seed, trunk bark, latex and feeder root of ten-year-old mature Reyan7-33-97 trees that had been tapped for two years, or from the latex collected from Reyan7-33-97 trees treated with 1.5% ethephon (2-chloroethylphosphonic acid, an ethylene generator) as described previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Tang, C. et al. The sucrose transporter HbSUT3 plays an active role in sucrose loading to laticifer and rubber productivity in exploited trees of Hevea brasiliensis (para rubber tree). Plant Cell Environ. 33, 1708–1720 (2010)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d17e2836">39</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Shotgun library construction and sequencing</h3><p>For genome sequencing, paired-end libraries (&lt;600 bp) were constructed by the standard A-tailing Illumina protocol. Mate-paired libraries (800 bp to 10 kb) were constructed by a modified SOLiD mate-pair library preparation protocol<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="van Heesch, S. et al. Improving mammalian genome scaffolding using large insert mate-pair next-generation sequencing. BMC Genomics 14, 257 (2013)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d17e2848">52</a></sup>. The Reyan7-33-97 genome was sequenced by the IlluminaGA2 and Hiseq2000 systems, whereas the genomes of re-sequenced cultivars were sequenced solely on the Hiseq2000 platform. Raw reads were preprocessed by an in-house perlscript, and then proofread by the ErrorCorrection module in the SOAPdenovo package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Li, R. et al. De novo assembly of human genomes with massively parallel short read sequencing. Genome Res. 20, 265–272 (2010)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d17e2852">53</a></sup>. For RNA-seq, RNA samples were used to prepare libraries and sequenced using the Illumina Hiseq2000 system. Raw RNA-seq reads were processed to trim terminal low quality bases and adapter sequences via an in-house custom pipeline. The clean reads were then mapped to the <i>Hevea</i> genome using GSNAP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Wu, T. D. &amp; Nacu, S. Fast and SNP-tolerant detection of complex variants and splicing in short reads. Bioinformatics 26, 873–881 (2010)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d17e2859">54</a></sup>. Cufflinks<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Trapnell, C. et al. Differential gene and transcript expression analysis of RNA-seq experiments with TopHat and Cufflinks. Nature Protoc. 7, 562–578 (2012)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d17e2863">55</a></sup> was used to compute the transcripts expression levels in reads per kilobase per million reads mapped (RPKM) and to identify the differentially expressed genes (DEGs) upon ethylene stimulation. Hierarchical clustering of DEGs was conducted using custom R scripts.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Genome assembly and validation</h3><p>The pre-processed paired-end reads were assembled using SOAPdenovo (69-mer size), and mate-pair reads were used to construct scaffolds using SSPACE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Boetzer, M., Henkel, C. V., Jansen, H. J., Butler, D. &amp; Pirovano, W. Scaffolding pre-assembled contigs using SSPACE. Bioinformatics 27, 578–579 (2011)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d17e2875">56</a></sup>. Scaffold gaps were closed with Gapfiller<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Nadalin, F., Vezzi, F. &amp; Policriti, A. GapFiller: a de novo assembly approach to fill the gap within paired reads. BMC bioinformatics 13 (Suppl. 14), S8 (2012)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d17e2879">57</a></sup> and the cd-hit software was employed to filter chimeric scaffolds<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Fu, L., Niu, B., Zhu, Z., Wu, S. &amp; Li, W. CD-HIT accelerated for clustering the next-generation sequencing data. Bioinformatics 28, 3150–3152 (2012)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d17e2883">58</a></sup>. A BAC-pooling sequencing strategy was employed to improve the assembly further (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#MOESM7">Supplementary Fig. 4</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#MOESM7">Supplementary Note 1</a>). Public databases, BAC and transcript sequences were used to validate the genome assembly (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#MOESM7">Supplementary Note 4</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Genome annotation</h3><p>Genome repeat sequences were annotated <i>de novo</i> by using RepeatMasker (<a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org">http://www.repeatmasker.org</a>). The protein-coding genes were predicted based on the repeat-masked genome through a combination of <i>ab initio</i>, conserved protein homologues and assembled transcripts. The non-coding RNAs were annotated by employing the INFERNAL software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Nawrocki, E. P. &amp; Eddy, S. R. Infernal 1.1: 100-fold faster RNA homology searches. Bioinformatics 29, 2933–2935 (2013)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d17e2918">59</a></sup> to search against the Rfam database (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#MOESM7">Supplementary Note 5</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Estimation of whole-genome duplication</h3><p>The all-to-all BLASTP program was used to identify the homolog pairs in <i>Hevea</i> proteins and the MCscan program<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Tang, H. et al. Synteny and collinearity in plant genomes. Science 320, 486–488 (2008)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d17e2937">60</a></sup> was used to search for collinearity blocks which were then filtered using the criterion of fewer than ten non-collinear genes between any two collinear genes. The same method was used to identify the syntenic blocks in <i>L. usitatissimum, P. trichocarpa, R. communis</i> and <i>M. esculenta,</i> or between <i>Hevea</i> and any of the four species. The collinearity region sizes for <i>Hevea</i>, <i>M. esculenta</i> and <i>P. trichocarpa</i> were estimated statistically by the Wilcox test. To estimate the duplication event, we calculated the synonymous <i>K</i><sub>s</sub> and fourfold synonymous third-codon transversion position (4DTv) using KaKs_calculator<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Zhang, Z. et al. KaKs_Calculator: calculating Ka and Ks through model selection and model averaging. Genomics Proteomics Bioinformatics 4, 259–263 (2006)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d17e2964">61</a></sup> with the NG model and an in-house perl script, respectively.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Phylogenetic analysis</h3><p>Twelve species, <i>Medicago truncatula, Glycine max, Fragaria vesca, Vitis vinifera, Oryza sativa, L. usitatissimum, P. trichocarpa, R. communis, M. esculenta, Citrus sinensis, Carica papaya</i> and <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>, were selected to construct a phylogenetic tree with <i>Hevea</i> using 72 single-copy gene families (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#MOESM7">Supplementary Note 7</a>). These species represent the typical Rosids sequenced to date in the Eudicots. <i>O. sativa</i> was designated as an outgroup.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Genetic heterogeneity of Hevea cultivars</h3><p>Paired-end sequences (35×) from each of the six <i>Hevea</i> cultivars were mapped to the Reyan7-33-97 reference genome using BWA (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Fast and accurate short read alignment with Burrows-Wheeler transform. Bioinformatics 25, 1754–1760 (2009)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d17e3001">62</a>). The mapping results were transformed into bam format and sorted with SAMtools<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Li, H. et al. The Sequence Alignment/Map format and SAMtools. Bioinformatics 25, 2078–2079 (2009)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d17e3005">63</a></sup>. SNP calling results were filtered against the following criteria: (1) each SNP supported by at least five non-redundant reads; (2) average mapping quality more than 40; and (3) two SNPs within 10 bp to be excluded. Based on the SNP calling results, SNP deserts (&lt;1 SNP per kb) were identified in each sequenced cultivar using a perl script. Genes located in SNP deserts were subjected to functional annotation and GO enrichment analysis using GOEAST (<a href="http://omicslab.genetics.ac.cn/GOEAST/">http://omicslab.genetics.ac.cn/GOEAST/</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Genes associated with rubber biosynthesis, ethylene synthesis and signalling</h3><p>The 20 gene families that have been reported as involved in rubber biosynthesis were identified from the genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#MOESM7">Supplementary Note 10</a>). Ethylene synthesis and signalling genes that had been characterized in <i>A. thaliana</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Zhao, Q. &amp; Guo, H. W. Paradigms and paradox in the ethylene signaling pathway and interaction network. Mol. Plant 4 626–634 (2011)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d17e3029">64</a></sup> were retrieved for their corresponding protein sequences from the Arabidopsis Information Resource (TAIR) (<a href="https://www.arabidopsis.org/">https://www.arabidopsis.org/</a> index.jsp). The retrieved <i>A. thaliana</i> proteins were processed with Interproscan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Li, W. et al. The EMBL-EBI bioinformatics web and programmatic tools framework. Nucleic Acids Res. 43, W580–W584 (2015)." href="/articles/nplants201673#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d17e3043">65</a></sup> and Blastp searched against <i>Hevea</i> proteins. Hits sharing &gt;30% amino acid identity and &gt;50% amino acid alignment length with the <i>A. thaliana</i> homologues were further checked for pfam domain architecture. Those having the same pfam domains as their <i>A. thaliana</i> homologues were regarded as proteins involved in ethylene synthesis and signalling in <i>Hevea</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201673#MOESM8">Supplementary Table 25</a>).</p><p>The <i>H. brasiliensis</i> genome and RNA-seq sequences have been deposited in GenBank/DDBJ/EMBL under the accession codes of LVXX01000000 and SRP069104, respectively.</p></div></div></section>

M.Y., Y.F., C.T., Y.L., S.H.G. and H.M. performed most of the data analysis. B.Z. and X.T. organized the experiments for genome- and BAC- sequencing, RNA-seq and library construction. X.X., B.Z., Z.A., Y.Q., J.H.Y., X.L., J.Q. and Y.H. performed the field experiments, DNA and RNA extraction, and PCR amplification for target genes. Q.L., P.M., Z.H., M.S., W.J.L., X.Z., H.C., Y.L., J.Y., W.T., N.Z., R.Z.Z., D.L., P.H., Z.L., Z.Z., S.L., C.L., J.W., D.W., C.Q.L. and W.L. were partially involved in either experiments or data analysis. C.T., M.Y., Y.F, Y.H.Y., J.Y. and S.H. wrote the manuscript. All authors discussed results and commented on the manuscript.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="corresponding-author">Corresponding authors</h3><p id="corresponding-author-list">Correspondence to
